    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">This can be a stand-alone book, but there is some wording in the final chapter that ties the book into the Galactic Empire novels that Asimov wrote. I<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">This can be a stand-alone book, but there is some wording in the final chapter that ties the book into the Galactic Empire novels that Asimov wrote. I'm not sure, though, if this book would be considered the first in the Galactic Empire saga.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook " href="/book/show/13345121-the-end-of-eternity">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=time-travel">time-travel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer158418807" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15139853702480216890">I've always felt that Isaac Asimov writes brilliant science fiction with boring characters. I love a good time travel story, mostly to see what this author's take on the usual time travel paradoxes will be. Anyone who writes about agents changing history has to explain how they deal with things like the Grandfather Paradox, meeting earlier or later versions of yourself, and so on. There are a handful of well-known ways to deal with these issues (alternate timelines, a deterministic universe, spe</span>
  <span id="freeText15139853702480216890" style="display:none">I've always felt that Isaac Asimov writes brilliant science fiction with boring characters. I love a good time travel story, mostly to see what this author's take on the usual time travel paradoxes will be. Anyone who writes about agents changing history has to explain how they deal with things like the Grandfather Paradox, meeting earlier or later versions of yourself, and so on. There are a handful of well-known ways to deal with these issues (alternate timelines, a deterministic universe, special laws of temporal physics, etc.) and Asimov is rather inventive in using several of them at once.<br /><br />Although <i>The End of Eternity</i> is brilliant in its construction of a civilization of time travelers and all the history and technology that goes into their society and the way they meddle with time, his protagonists are basically a bunch of whiny geeks who've never kissed a girl and act like highly-educated chimpanzees fighting for the highest branch in the treehouse. Asimov's vision of a civilization that spans millions of years and thousands of realities doesn't include a single one where women become scientists and engineers and might join the Eternals' boys' club. The entire plot hinges on not one but two high-ranking Eternals who decide they are willing to throw all of reality into danger for the chance to get laid. I know Asimov was a nerd and he wrote this in the 1950s, but he still could have done better. It's like the idea of women as anything but sex objects to be coveted or to seduce men off the path of Righteous Scientific Objectivity just never occurred to him. So naturally when a girl shows up (the only female character in the entire book), she must spell T-R-O-U-B-L-E, and in this case, the end of Eternity.<br /><br />I enjoyed the story, but Asimov has never been my favorite among the Grand Old Masters of science fiction; there is something just a little too cold and calculating in all of his stories. For the ideas and the plot twists, this is a fun book with a great premise, but don't expect Asimov to wow you with his nuanced grasp of human relationships. His characters are wire dummies to hang a story on.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15139853702480216890" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1113128638" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1113128638" />
    <a title="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl"><img alt="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603085284p2/8734459.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1113128638?book_show_action=true">Nov 22, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl">Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=time-travel">time-travel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=oldies-but-goodies">oldies-but-goodies</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1113128638" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4403492748389968625">Final review, first posted on <a href='http://www.fantasyliterature.com' rel='nofollow noopener'>Fantasy Literature</a>:<br/><br/>Re-reading a favorite book from your teenage years is always a risky endeavor. I’ve been dismayed by how often my youthful memories are tarnished by a re-read, and I end up wondering if my taste as a young adult was all in my mouth.<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1420012593i/13154145.jpg' width='250' height='155' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>But I couldn’t resist trying <i>The End of Eternity</i> (1955) by Isaac Asimov again, partly because I remembered liking it so well as a teenager, but my memories of it were so extremely hazy (for the longest time, until a Goo</span>
  <span id="freeText4403492748389968625" style="display:none">Final review, first posted on <a href="http://www.fantasyliterature.com" rel="nofollow noopener">Fantasy Literature</a>:<br><br>Re-reading a favorite book from your teenage years is always a risky endeavor. I’ve been dismayed by how often my youthful memories are tarnished by a re-read, and I end up wondering if my taste as a young adult was all in my mouth.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1420012593i/13154145.jpg" width="250" height="155" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>But I couldn’t resist trying <i>The End of Eternity</i> (1955) by Isaac Asimov again, partly because I remembered liking it so well as a teenager, but my memories of it were so extremely hazy (for the longest time, until a Google search saved me, I couldn’t even remember the title of the book, it was just “that really cool Asimov time-traveling book” in my head). So I bought a used copy, got a few chuckles out of the 1970s sci-fi cover and how short novels used to be (192 pages here), and settled down to read.<br><br>Andrew Harlan is one of the so-called Eternals, men (almost invariably men) who have been pulled into kind of a bubble called “Eternity” that exists outside of normal time. Eternity, and a time-traveling machine called the “Kettle” that acts as a type of elevator through the years of the Earth’s existence, give them the ability to easily travel backwards and forwards in time. Eternals can change the past, present and future, which they frequently do when they think that society is taking a turn that leads to an undesirable outcome. Strangely, however, the Kettle is inexplicably blocked from stopping anywhere during the 70,000th to the 150,000th centuries, and afterwards lies only emptiness: Earth and its inhabitants are gone.<br><br>Love and marriage aren’t permitted for Eternals, other than brief sexual liaisons that are required to go through authorized channels. But Andrew, despite his best efforts to avoid it, manages to fall into a relationship, and then love, with a woman, Nöyes Lambert, who has been temporarily brought into Eternity. Soon he finds himself in the middle of not only his own small personal rebellion, but also a series of events that may affect Eternity and change the entire history of the Earth.<br><br>I’m vastly relieved to report that <i>The End of Eternity</i> has held up quite well over the decades. It’s certainly dated, and for a while I thought I was going to have some fairly serious issues with the secondary role of women in this novel, but that all actually resolved itself quite well in the end (though to explain why would spoil the tale).<br><br><i>The End of Eternity</i> has the retro charm of 1950s science fiction, but with more depth than most sci-fi novels from that age. It has its weaknesses: Asimov’s scientific theory and technology for time travel are a little wild and woolly, female characters are non-existent other than Nöyes, and all of the characters except (to some extent) Andrew and Nöyes are strictly one-dimensional. If you can roll with it, however, it’s a fun and interesting ride, with a few twists and turns that definitely make the story memorable.<br><br><b>Initial review:</b> I've been asking myself for ages, what was that time travel story of Isaac Asimov's that I loved when I was a teenager? and the question suddenly became more urgent after an interesting discussion I was having with some GR friends about time travel novels we've liked (see the thread to Joe Valdez's review of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18619684.The_Time_Traveler_s_Wife" title="The Time Traveler's Wife by Audrey Niffenegger" rel="nofollow noopener">The Time Traveler's Wife</a>). And it occurred to me to Google "Isaac Asimov time travel," duh, and there it was. <br><br>So the four stars here are based purely on my love for this book ages ago, and unfortunately I've found that my teenage taste in books is not always a reliable indicator of literary quality, so don't blame me if you read this and think it's a dud. I'd like to read it again, but I just checked and my local library doesn't have it on their shelves (maybe ILL? Will have to see sometime) so it'll probably be a while before I re-read this. But Uncle Isaac was a big reason for my teenage love for science fiction, which has lasted for my entire life. Just for that, he gets as many stars as I care to dish out. No apologies.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4403492748389968625" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_39047891" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/39047891" />
    <a title="Manny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1713956-manny"><img alt="Manny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442491692p2/1713956.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/39047891?book_show_action=true">Dec 01, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Manny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Manny" href="/user/show/1713956-manny">Manny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer39047891" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5603133636051780224">If you haven't read Asimov's SF classic, it's one of those time-travel stories where you can change the past. The people with the time machines are a shadowy, infinitely powerful organisation called the Eternals. They flit around in time, changing things "for the good of humanity". Except that, as I'm sure you already guessed, it isn't quite clear after a while that humanity is benefiting from all this attention. The agents who are responsible for making the changes are called Technicians, and t</span>
  <span id="freeText5603133636051780224" style="display:none">If you haven't read Asimov's SF classic, it's one of those time-travel stories where you can change the past. The people with the time machines are a shadowy, infinitely powerful organisation called the Eternals. They flit around in time, changing things "for the good of humanity". Except that, as I'm sure you already guessed, it isn't quite clear after a while that humanity is benefiting from all this attention. The agents who are responsible for making the changes are called Technicians, and they pride themselves on always finding the very simplest way to effect the change. They don't start a war if it's enough just to assassinate one key person; they don't assassinate him if it's enough just to organise a traffic accident so that he misses a meeting; and they don't organise the traffic accident if it's enough just to put his address book in a different pocket, so that he makes the critical phone call twenty minutes too late. In a word, they're minimalists.<br /><br />I was talking about this book the other day with an American friend who'd also read it. If the premise of the story really were true, we wondered what evidence you could find to suggest that Eternals had been at work. We couldn't help thinking that the 2000 Butterfly Ballot was suspicious. Not least the name - perhaps some Technician had been unable to resist the joke, and planted a larger clue than he really was supposed to? <br /><br />__________________________________<br /><br />After posting this review and that for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/39047809" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>The Naked Sun</i>
</a>, I did some more googling on the background to <i>Eternity</i>. Among other things, I discovered that Asimov claimed he got the original idea when he saw an ad in an early 30s newspaper, showing a picture that looked rather like a mushroom cloud. Well, he thought to himself, no one in 1930 knew what a mushroom cloud looked like. Maybe it's a message from a stranded time-traveller? And, from that beginning, he constructed the rest of the novel.<br /><br />I liked it, but I was dubious about the reliability of the method. Time travel probably won't be invented very soon - it sounds like extremely advanced technology. If you really did want to send a message into the distant future, you'd want something far more permanent and noticeable than an obscure ad. Something, in fact, that would have a decent chance of surviving for thousands of years, in unchanged form, and which would be as prominent as possible. The ideal thing would be an immortal work of art.<br /><br />And then it struck me: in a different thread, we'd been talking about Shakespeare's mysterious Sonnet XVIII. Ladies and gentlemen of the jury, I submit to your august consideration the hypothesis that this is a message from a time-traveller who crashed their machine in the early 17th century. To start off with, the poem explicitly says that its central purpose is to be remembered for ever. It's so beautiful, and every word is so perfectly chosen, that it has a decent chance of surviving unchanged for thousands of years - maybe, even until people get around to inventing time travel? <br /><br />If you want to turn this into a short story, please credit me somewhere :)<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5603133636051780224" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_570499537" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/570499537" />
    <a title="Bradley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley"><img alt="Bradley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508811508p2/4213258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/570499537?book_show_action=true">May 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=2018-shelf">2018-shelf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer570499537" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6559495675400880229">I just had to do a little retro SF catch-up, grabbing those old classics by big-name SF authors that I haven't yet had the pleasure to read, and this one kept cropping up as one of the best of the best by Asimov. <br/><br/>You know, OTHER than Foundation and the Robot novels. Of which, a few are sub-par. We'll ignore all of these for now and focus on this standalone.<br/><br/>About time travel in a kettle, kinda like Wells' time machine, only let's make a society of men, only men, living outside of time a-la Time L</span>
  <span id="freeText6559495675400880229" style="display:none">I just had to do a little retro SF catch-up, grabbing those old classics by big-name SF authors that I haven't yet had the pleasure to read, and this one kept cropping up as one of the best of the best by Asimov. <br /><br />You know, OTHER than Foundation and the Robot novels. Of which, a few are sub-par. We'll ignore all of these for now and focus on this standalone.<br /><br />About time travel in a kettle, kinda like Wells' time machine, only let's make a society of men, only men, living outside of time a-la Time Lords and have our MC be a pre-Doctor kind of character who's ACTUALLY willing to fall in love with a girl and is willing to DESTROY this little bubble of Eternity for her sake. <br /><br />You know, because a society of nothing but men will obviously think with nothing more than the spout of their kettles.<br /><br />And don't get me started on this 50's assumption that only men can do the work, but because even Asimov recognized all that and turned her into a femme fatale and made the girls more badass than the boys. So we learn. LATER. :) So let's move on from there.<br /><br />This is basically Doctor Who on steroids and less buddy-buddy unique eras and a hardcore dive into escaping a whole society of time travelers who meddle with the past to erase the really bad stuff, fixing whole timelines on massive scales over vast time periods... ALL FOR THE SAKE OF TIPPING HIS TEAKETTLE.<br /><br />Oh, and he decides it's okay to destroy all the Time Lords. Ahem. Sorry. Eternals.<br /><br />What could have been a relatively average and not bad at all novel right HERE is then given the full Asimov twist and he turns it into a full adventure with deeper and deeper intrigue, reversals, surprises, reveals, and mystery. Not bad, Asimov. And then he even goes for the short-story twist at the end and makes us re-evaluate EVERYTHING that has happened before in a new light.<br /><br />NOT BAD AT ALL.<br /><br />So if you can get over the naming conventions and the cardboard cutout characters and the whole psychosexual mess, I can ABSOLUTELY PROMISE YOU that there's a very fine and fun novel in here. :) Worthy of anything we've got today and somewhat more ambitious, even with the length, than most of the same. <br /><br />I'm very glad to have read this. :)</span>
  <a data-text-id="6559495675400880229" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_462369635" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/462369635" />
    <a title="sologdin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5610133-sologdin"><img alt="sologdin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307928658p2/5610133.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/462369635?book_show_action=true">Nov 24, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="sologdin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="sologdin" href="/user/show/5610133-sologdin">sologdin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553288094" href="/book/show/1133206.The_End_of_Eternity">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5610133-sologdin?shelf=burns-like-cold-iron">burns-like-cold-iron</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer462369635" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17726640884387770618">Nutshell: antisocial nerd, responsible for historical amendments to spacetime continuum, dicks it up for everyone in order to lose virginity.<br/><br/><i>Eternity</i> is an interdimensional NGO, set up in the 27th century (32), initially to carry on intertemporal trade (43), which trade was promoted as its primary purpose. Its true primary task is to "prevent catastrophe from striking mankind" and "to breed out of Reality any factors that might lead to such knowledge" of its biotemporal management of human histo</span>
  <span id="freeText17726640884387770618" style="display:none">Nutshell: antisocial nerd, responsible for historical amendments to spacetime continuum, dicks it up for everyone in order to lose virginity.<br /><br /><i>Eternity</i> is an interdimensional NGO, set up in the 27th century (32), initially to carry on intertemporal trade (43), which trade was promoted as its primary purpose. Its true primary task is to "prevent catastrophe from striking mankind" and "to breed out of Reality any factors that might lead to such knowledge" of its biotemporal management of human history (43-44).<br /><br />We see that the main component of biotemporal management is actually wealth management for each century: "The Sociologists had an equation for the phenomenon" of uneven wealth distribution (45). Biotemporal managers allowed aristocracies to form, so long as they "did not entirely forget their responsibilities while enjoying their privileges" (46). Analogues to marxism here to the extent that the managers viewed the aristocracy as a ruling entity, "a class, not as individuals" (38). Generally, the point is to protect the species from destroying itself in nuclear war, but there's talk in the NGO of abolishing space travel, which always turns out to be a disaster.<br /><br />Some odd gender politics: no women in the NGO, for the bizarre reason that removing females from the spacetime continuum actually has a more deleterious effect than removing males (something to do with the birthrate). Plenty of commentary, express and implied, on freedom &amp; determinism. Strikes me that determinism is the default position when the premise of the story is that changes initiated by the managers at one point alter later effects. That said, some characters believe in "temporal inertia" (169), and that effects from changes return to a hypothetical baseline after a nunber of centuries, rather than creating further diremptions.<br /><br />Anyway, lotsa paradoxes, including the central paradox of the novel (or, rather, of the Setting, rather than the Story): how is it that changes to history do not effect the NGO when the NGO interacts with and draws from history? <br /><br />Nifty link toward the end to the Robot/Empire/Foundation setting: safe to say that the denouement is the condition of possibility for that narrative.<br /><br />Recommended for those who stumble upon temporal field theory without being aware of its mathematical justification, persons for whom human appetites carry a quivery repulsion, and readers who associate the mushroom cloud with the system by which private capital was invested in business.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17726640884387770618" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_12133728" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/12133728" />
    <a title="Ray" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/765636-ray"><img alt="Ray" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546566068p2/765636.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/12133728?book_show_action=true">Jan 10, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ray" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ray" href="/user/show/765636-ray">Ray</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/765636-ray?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/765636-ray?shelf=audio">audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/765636-ray?shelf=sci-fic">sci-fic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer12133728" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2008185828321251917">In many ways, an absolute masterpiece of classic science fiction. Asimov's best stand-alone novel, which is kind of by default due to his other great work Foundation being a series and most of his other science fiction works to be short stories. (And yes, I know The End of Eternity in an indirect way does connect to Foundation.)<br/><br/>The time travel mechanics are mind-boggling in all the best ways. The concept of Eternity which manipulates time century by century, erasing entire realities by constantl</span>
  <span id="freeText2008185828321251917" style="display:none">In many ways, an absolute masterpiece of classic science fiction. Asimov's best stand-alone novel, which is kind of by default due to his other great work Foundation being a series and most of his other science fiction works to be short stories. (And yes, I know The End of Eternity in an indirect way does connect to Foundation.)<br /><br />The time travel mechanics are mind-boggling in all the best ways. The concept of Eternity which manipulates time century by century, erasing entire realities by constantly making slight edits, is certainly fascinating. Even over sixty years later, the book mainly holds up extremely well and few contemporary works could compare to these brilliant concepts.<br /><br />However, in a more social and literary sense, there is unfortunately a lot to criticize. Although the book imagines far off centuries thousands upon thousands of years in the future, it just can't get over the sexism of the 1950s era in which it was written. The main character, in a bit of a cliche, questions everything after he falls in love with a woman who will be erased if he doesn't fight the power in Eternity. <br /><br />This specifically exposes the central problem of the narrative: how shocking it is for this imagined council of time lords to have any women around. Why is that? There are some throwaway lines about how female Eternals are rare because women are so important to the timelines they're from that they can't be taken out, and also the love interest character does turn out to be more important and powerful in the end. But come on, the real reason Eternity is such a sausage fest is because Asimov just couldn't get over the idea that only men can be super-scientists. And that just don't age well. <br /><br />I'll still give this a full 5 star review because it is a classic and is a product of its time--even if as a time travel story the author could have tried harder to craft some more open-minded futurism.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2008185828321251917" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14674839694764664870">This is a unique one for Asimov, and not connected to his usual fictional universe. Ironically, this is his first book I tried to read, <i>in Malayalam translation</i>, no less! But either the translation was bad, or the story was untranslatable, or I was too young for it... I dropped it after a few pages. I am glad I did, because I could read the original afresh.<br/><br/>This story is about a group called Eternals who travel outside of linear time, stepping in when required within the time-stream to make thing</span>
  <span id="freeText14674839694764664870" style="display:none">This is a unique one for Asimov, and not connected to his usual fictional universe. Ironically, this is his first book I tried to read, <i>in Malayalam translation</i>, no less! But either the translation was bad, or the story was untranslatable, or I was too young for it... I dropped it after a few pages. I am glad I did, because I could read the original afresh.<br /><br />This story is about a group called Eternals who travel outside of linear time, stepping in when required within the time-stream to make things "better for humanity". Well, it all ends up rather like America making the world safe for democracy. <br /><br />I don't remember much other than the main character almost meeting himself as he enters the time-stream from different points. For some reason, this is considered a disaster. I don't know why. If I get chance, I would like to go forward and meet my older self, just to know how I made out.</span>

<div id="review_554719151" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/554719151" />
    <a title="Rose" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7891507-rose"><img alt="Rose" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1443344607p2/7891507.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/554719151?book_show_action=true">Jan 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rose" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rose" href="/user/show/7891507-rose">Rose</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7891507-rose?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7891507-rose?shelf=time-travel">time-travel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer554719151" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1301615968161127455">Even though I tend to pick them apart, I love stories dealing with time-travel. Most end up creating what should be a paradox or forget little things that I tend to pick up on. Not this one. Asimov did a spectacular job. I thought I had him a couple of times but he covered everything.<br/><br/>Eternity, in this case, is something separate from our reality. The people who live there, the Eternals, have the ability to tweak our reality to produce different outcomes from those that occurred naturally. They s</span>
  <span id="freeText1301615968161127455" style="display:none">Even though I tend to pick them apart, I love stories dealing with time-travel. Most end up creating what should be a paradox or forget little things that I tend to pick up on. Not this one. Asimov did a spectacular job. I thought I had him a couple of times but he covered everything.<br /><br />Eternity, in this case, is something separate from our reality. The people who live there, the Eternals, have the ability to tweak our reality to produce different outcomes from those that occurred naturally. They start tweaking from the 2700’s going right up to the 70,000’s. Things ran smoothly for a long time until Eternal Harlan meets a girl. Everything always gets mucked up because of us, right? So Harlan decides he must have this girl, but because he’s not allowed he progressively gets into more trouble. Harlan’s real problem is that he doesn’t have all the information, nor do we until the very end, so he keeps messing things up and then has to fix them...until he has to break it all in the biggest way.<br /><br />The writing was dated but not horribly so. Women were still not equals, but I think in the end we actually were. There wasn’t a lot of speculation as to how we would live in the future as the story focussed mainly on Eternity rather than reality, but it gave enough to make you dream of where humanity might be headed. A definite must-read for time-travel lovers!!</span>
  <a data-text-id="1301615968161127455" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_155530262" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/155530262" />
    <a title="Mark Harding" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4238828-mark-harding"><img alt="Mark Harding" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1283457632p2/4238828.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer8184642884122111274">Less than 200 pages. The same material nowadays would be expanded to at least 600 pages or probably a trilogy. <br/><br/>Well cool ideas:<br/><br/>* The time shafts are powered by our Sun going supernova in the distant future.<br/>* Idealogical debate between Utilitarianism and ‘adventure’.   <span id="freeText8184642884122111274" style="display:none">Less than 200 pages. The same material nowadays would be expanded to at least 600 pages or probably a trilogy. <br /><br />Well cool ideas:<br /><br />* The time shafts are powered by our Sun going supernova in the distant future.<br />* Idealogical debate between Utilitarianism and ‘adventure’. (And fighting for frontiers) or ‘short term’ safety vs risk and long term growth.<br />* Everything that happens is intricately played out according to the central plot driver<br /><br />I suppose because SF writers feel obliged to provide ‘characters’ and ‘texture’ and ‘a character journey’ this sort of stripped-down relating of plot and ideas wouldn’t be written now. Sure, characters and all that stuff is good, but there’s an attractive clarity of purpose to this book - especially when the plot becomes increasingly complex as the story progresses.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10265639033845541417">Asimov is doing a couple of things here that raise a dull story to interesting, but you won't be able to catch it until the final two chapters. Dare I say this is a feminist critique of society?... I mean, as critical as a '50s dude from sexist academia could deliver? And delivered in quite possibly the most offensive way possible, at least to modern sensibilities? It partly serves as a polemic against critics of the space program (I smell a colleague confrontation brewing, Asi), partly an effor</span>
  <span id="freeText10265639033845541417" style="display:none">Asimov is doing a couple of things here that raise a dull story to interesting, but you won't be able to catch it until the final two chapters. Dare I say this is a feminist critique of society?... I mean, as critical as a '50s dude from sexist academia could deliver? And delivered in quite possibly the most offensive way possible, at least to modern sensibilities? It partly serves as a polemic against critics of the space program (I smell a colleague confrontation brewing, Asi), partly an effort to challenge the '50s male view of, ahem, "girls," but mostly just a lame time travel romance with a pretty cool twist ending (almost exactly the kind my cynical self always hopes will happen in romance stories but never does). BUT, is this the very first time a time travel story employs magazine ads as communication between eras? If so, that's pretty cool!</span>

<div id="review_403388849" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/403388849" />
    <a title="Amy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/563794-amy"><img alt="Amy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603051442p2/563794.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/403388849?book_show_action=true">Aug 29, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amy" href="/user/show/563794-amy">Amy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/563794-amy?shelf=2012-books-read">2012-books-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/563794-amy?shelf=time-travel">time-travel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/563794-amy?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer403388849" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9203069527214146753">This is somehow my first Asimov book. At first, I was underwhelmed. However, as the book went forward, I found that there was much more depth to the writing than originally met the eye and that the shallow characters were shallow with a purpose. <br/><br/>Asimov sets up a group of scientist who are outside of time called the Eternals. While everyone on earth thinks that their main job is to facilitate commerce between various centuries, their true function is to manipulate history to make it play out more</span>
  <span id="freeText9203069527214146753" style="display:none">This is somehow my first Asimov book. At first, I was underwhelmed. However, as the book went forward, I found that there was much more depth to the writing than originally met the eye and that the shallow characters were shallow with a purpose. <br /><br />Asimov sets up a group of scientist who are outside of time called the Eternals. While everyone on earth thinks that their main job is to facilitate commerce between various centuries, their true function is to manipulate history to make it play out more favorably. Thus, the Eternals have become gods without the people of the world knowing it. They've become puppeteers to a population that doesn't know to fight back, thus leaving the Eternals seemingly untouchable. It makes for an interesting polytheocracy where the people aren't even aware that their scientist have turned themselves into gods. <br /><br />It seems that only the brightest, most analytical, most unquestioning, most inexperienced in life, and most naive need apply for the job of Eternal. Recruiters would need to find someone who lived their entire life with those qualities to hopefully ensure that they'd not eventually change into someone who would call for a revolution in Eternity because they could no longer agree with the level of control and puppetmastery that the Eternals wield. With such a combination of personality traits, Our Hero falls in love with the first scantily clad woman that throws herself his way. And, in so doing, he puts the very existence of Eternity in peril because he's willing to do whatever it takes to keep her. And upon this small rebellion, our story turns.<br /><br />An interesting mystery is that the Eternals are shielded from being able to visit the future beyond a certain point. Apparently, future humans don't want the daliance of the Eternals in their affairs. Asimov makes the point that species evolve to adapt to their environment. However, since humans are able to adapt their environments to suit them, he postulates that humans have and will continue to evolve at much slower rates than beings that are unable to modify their environments. So we guess that future humans aren't that much different genetically from present-day humans. One wonders. My next read is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/264946.Hominids__Neanderthal_Parallax___1_" title="Hominids (Neanderthal Parallax, #1) by Robert J. Sawyer" rel="nofollow noopener">Hominids</a> (a book I've been wanting for ages which I received as a birthday gift) which imagines a parallel universe where Neanderthals did not die out or intermix with homo sapiens and eventually became more technologically advanced than we have during the same time period. I have a strong intrigue concerning the what-ifs of our evolutionary might-have-beens or our evolutionary future. I think fiction like this appeals to me simply because I only have one life to live and will never see our evolutionary future thousands or millions of years from now beyond an author's imagination. I'd love to see a far distant future as imagined by Asimov. <br /><br />I'll certainly read Asimov again. The content of the story was merely okay, but the questions that he created for me while I was reading have left me wanting more. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9203069527214146753" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_55236034" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/55236034" />
    <a title="Anand Srinivas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2294666-anand-srinivas"><img alt="Anand Srinivas" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/55236034?book_show_action=true">May 07, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anand Srinivas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anand Srinivas" href="/user/show/2294666-anand-srinivas">Anand Srinivas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294666-anand-srinivas?shelf=asimov">asimov</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer55236034" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9664059088977945692">I'm a SF buff, a big Asimov fan, and I'd rate this book among his very best. Having read through most of Asimov's Robot and Foundation series, what drew me to read The End of Eternity was the often mentioned fact that this book describes why Asimov's fictional universe panned out the way it did. And I wasn't disappointed. What a story!<br/><br/>One of the things I like most about Asimov's SF is how small and insignificant it makes you feel when it speaks about millions of inhabited planets spread across t</span>
  <span id="freeText9664059088977945692" style="display:none">I'm a SF buff, a big Asimov fan, and I'd rate this book among his very best. Having read through most of Asimov's Robot and Foundation series, what drew me to read The End of Eternity was the often mentioned fact that this book describes why Asimov's fictional universe panned out the way it did. And I wasn't disappointed. What a story!<br /><br />One of the things I like most about Asimov's SF is how small and insignificant it makes you feel when it speaks about millions of inhabited planets spread across the galaxy. This book makes me feel insignificant again, but in an uniquely different way as it speaks about life on earth alone divided into 'centuries' of time, in a way similar to having thousands of different planets. The 'Eternals' who traverse and curate these chunks of time eventually find the flaw in their own methods, and following a theme that exists across many of Asimov's stories, let the uncontrollable imperfections of humanity that make it human win.<br /><br />A must read for Asimov fans.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9664059088977945692" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2406774414" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10912628475973072205"><strong>Time Travel! </strong><br/><br/>I am a huge fan of time travel, particularly when it's done well. And, should I expect anything less in the hands of Asimov? Long before we learned how to harness the pathways of time and to make the proper adjustments to each time reality, Asimov bequeathed to us this incredible novel that postulates a world where there me travel exists in a corridor known as Eternity and the Eternals, who live in this narrow corridor, travel tens of thousands of years in something like Wonka's gia</span>
  <span id="freeText10912628475973072205" style="display:none"><strong>Time Travel! </strong><br /><br />I am a huge fan of time travel, particularly when it's done well. And, should I expect anything less in the hands of Asimov? Long before we learned how to harness the pathways of time and to make the proper adjustments to each time reality, Asimov bequeathed to us this incredible novel that postulates a world where there me travel exists in a corridor known as Eternity and the Eternals, who live in this narrow corridor, travel tens of thousands of years in something like Wonka's giant glass elevators. They call them kettles. But, Asimov doesn't stop there with time travel tourism. In Eternity, the Eternals curate time. They analyze alternate realities and prevent great calamities like cancer or atomic war. A little adjustment now and then never hurt anybody. <br /><br />But what if one of the Eternals meets a mere mortal in a time existence and falls for her many charms? Not hard to do because like the bunch of eggheads the science fiction boys were, there was always a "no girls allowed" sign out. And how does he protect this young lass from time adjustments that just might wink her out of existence? Where can he hide her? How does he escape notice with this time crime? <br /><br />This is a brilliant wonderful book that only gets better the deeper you dive into it with all the time traps, time dilemmas, and paradoxical circles you can think up. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10912628475973072205" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_397411788" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/397411788" />
    <a title="Lynley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4278829-lynley"><img alt="Lynley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442509376p2/4278829.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/397411788?book_show_action=true">Aug 21, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lynley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lynley" href="/user/show/4278829-lynley">Lynley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4278829-lynley?shelf=america">america</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4278829-lynley?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer397411788" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2647720737823629665">5 stars for the start, 2 stars for the middle, 5 stars for the ending.<br/><br/>This book has among the best beginnings I've ever read. I must have picked this book up about 10 years ago but it wasn't my book so I only read the first chapter or so. That first chapter stayed in my mind long after I'd forgotten the title, but in a commitment to find out what happened next, I finally sniffed out this Isaac Asimov book and read the rest.<br/><br/>Naturally, an impression such as that was never going to live up to my ex</span>
  <span id="freeText2647720737823629665" style="display:none">5 stars for the start, 2 stars for the middle, 5 stars for the ending.<br /><br />This book has among the best beginnings I've ever read. I must have picked this book up about 10 years ago but it wasn't my book so I only read the first chapter or so. That first chapter stayed in my mind long after I'd forgotten the title, but in a commitment to find out what happened next, I finally sniffed out this Isaac Asimov book and read the rest.<br /><br />Naturally, an impression such as that was never going to live up to my expectations. From what I've read about Isaac Asimov the author, he was fairly forward looking for his time, and not just in scientific thinking. One of his non-fiction books is called 'The Intelligent Man's Guide To Science', which today seems hopelessly sexist, but apparently Asimov himself didn't want such a sexist title. Unfortunately, the publisher had the final say, because that's just how things were in those days. <br /><br />In this story, a small part of me is sad that Asimov couldn't imagine a workforce which included women as equals. I know, it seems churlish to even bring this point up, because the whole point of the book is that humankind failed to evolve. But even today there is a subculture of men who still react as Andrew Harlan did to an attractive woman in the workforce, blaming her for attracting him because of the fashion of the day. This was a necessary part of the plot, but it conjured up unpleasant political feelings that I'd been hoping to avoid in a work of science fiction. Even a mind such as Asimov's failed to predict how much feminism would advance in the few short years immediately following his death. That's not the only thing he failed to predict and it seems pointless criticising him for any of it, in a way.<br /><br />The plot got a bit complex halfway through and I'd stopped focusing on the nit-picky details after a while, because this is a story after all. According to actual science, time travel to the past isn't even theoretically possible, so I went all out and suspended disbelief of everything. (I'm sure SF fans are well-used to doing this, but I still have my training wheels.) This skipping over the details meant that I lost the plot a bit. My main problem, though, was with the concept of human evolution. I suspect humans are devolving physically while evolving culturally. That is entirely possible. Those two different types of evolution weren't separated, instead lumped into one.<br /><br />The ending was superb. I was really wondering how Asimov might tie things up in a satisfactory way, and he did it. A few years ago I read The Never-Ending Days Of Being Dead by Marcus Chown, which blew my mind. Had I not already read that, and been introduced to the idea of infinite realities, this book may have blown my mind too.<br /><br />(Completely unsurprising) Update:<br /><br />"author Isaac Asimov's proclivity for groping women was so widely known that in 1961, the chair of Chicon III wrote a letter inviting him to give a lecture on "The Power of Posterior Pinching"<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.gizmodo.com.au/2016/08/dont-look-away-fighting-sexual-harassment-in-the-scififantasy-community/">https://www.gizmodo.com.au/2016/08/do...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="2647720737823629665" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1670770451" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1670770451" />
    <a title="Eric Allen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6229358-eric-allen"><img alt="Eric Allen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560117879p2/6229358.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1670770451?book_show_action=true">Jun 16, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eric Allen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eric Allen" href="/user/show/6229358-eric-allen">Eric Allen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1670770451" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3566459319398934066">I recently watched a TV series called Steins;gate. It started out as a lighthearted comedy, where a self-proclaimed mad scientist creates a time machine that allows him to send text messages into the past, by making the plate in his microwave turn backwards. It was really funny and entertaining, and then it went really, really dark as all of the consequences to changes in the past that they made with the machine started hitting, and they spent the rest of the series trying to undo or otherwise f</span>
  <span id="freeText3566459319398934066" style="display:none">I recently watched a TV series called Steins;gate. It started out as a lighthearted comedy, where a self-proclaimed mad scientist creates a time machine that allows him to send text messages into the past, by making the plate in his microwave turn backwards. It was really funny and entertaining, and then it went really, really dark as all of the consequences to changes in the past that they made with the machine started hitting, and they spent the rest of the series trying to undo or otherwise fix all of the changes that they'd made. <br /><br />Pretty much through the entire series I kept thinking of this book, because Steins;gate really seemed like a m0dernized version of the story. I remembered really enjoying this book when I was in high school, but that was twenty years ago, and early 70s sci-fi usually leaves much to be desired in this day and age. I think a lot of people will agree that this book was pretty influential in the sci-fi community at large over the years. You'll find nods to it in the Back to the Future movies, Timecop, Bill and Ted, and other such time travel movies. And Timelord society in the Tom Baker years of Doctor Who was largely based upon the Eternals in this book. But sometimes, when you go back to the source, it wasn't as good as you remember it being.<br /><br />In my opinion, this is Asimov's best book. It's slightly better than Foundation, and definitely better than Robots. It hasn't really aged very well, however. It's still a pretty thought-provoking story, but Asimov is pretty dismissive of women, using them as stage props rather than characters, and his characters usually make beige walls look interesting by comparison. It is very much a product of the time in which it was written. Asimov grew up in a world where women were seen as inferior to men, and you can see this attitude in pretty much all of his books. I'm not making excuses for him, just giving explanation as I see it. And I am not, by any means, saying that there's perfect equality between the sexes even now, but we're a far cry closer than we were fifty years ago when this book was written. If this book were to be submitted to a publisher in this day and age, it would likely be tossed out with all the other rubbish submissions they receive. It's still a solid three star book, that I bumped up to four for nostalgia value. If you would like a pretty cool time travel story with a lot of interesting ideas, and don't care that it, in no way comes close to passing the Bechdel Test, you may enjoy this book. If you're looking for a time travel story with actual characters in it, skip The End of Eternity, and go watch Steins;gate instead. Because, seriously, that show was great.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3566459319398934066" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2095356711" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2095356711" />
    <a title="Kevin Kuhn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59568642-kevin-kuhn"><img alt="Kevin Kuhn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508679965p2/59568642.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2095356711?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kevin Kuhn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kevin Kuhn" href="/user/show/59568642-kevin-kuhn">Kevin Kuhn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59568642-kevin-kuhn?shelf=time-travel">time-travel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2095356711" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9319423617173924054">Lately, I’ve been a bit nostalgic for classic sci-fi and I’ve been reading some Heinlein and Asimov. I somehow missed this book when I tore through all the Asimov novels way back in my youth. I enjoyed this book, but it does have a significant flaw. The basic premise is mankind’s establishment of Eternity in the 27th century that allowed trade of goods from one century to another. However, those that controlled Eternity also controlled human history. <br/><br/>	I loved the big concepts in this novel. The </span>
  <span id="freeText9319423617173924054" style="display:none">Lately, I’ve been a bit nostalgic for classic sci-fi and I’ve been reading some Heinlein and Asimov. I somehow missed this book when I tore through all the Asimov novels way back in my youth. I enjoyed this book, but it does have a significant flaw. The basic premise is mankind’s establishment of Eternity in the 27th century that allowed trade of goods from one century to another. However, those that controlled Eternity also controlled human history. <br /><br />	I loved the big concepts in this novel. The idea of constructing a structure outside of time, that allows people (Eternals) to monitor and manage mankind is a fun thought experiment. The storyline is good as well, the main character does what no Eternal is supposed to do, fall in love. There is some mystery as well, with some hidden centuries and uncertainty about how eternity was established. I loved the ending, which had a major twist that surprised me. It also got me thinking which, for me, is the sign of a great ending. So, big picture, great novel, with one flaw.<br />	The flaw is the same one I’m seeing in a great number of classic sci fi. Of course, this is well known, I’m just being reminded of it. In this novel, largely only men control and work in eternity. Women occasionally are allowed to cook, clean and do other menial tasks. No kidding, this is the plot. The main character falls in love with a women, mainly because she’s beautiful and he’s had little exposure to women. Their relationship develops because she’s sexy and he’s unable to help himself. There is no real development of their relationship other than physical attraction. For me, it makes the relationship flat and lessens the novel. If you don’t think we have made at least some progress in equality of the sexes, just go back and read some 1950’s science fiction. <br /><br />	Ultimately, I was able to set this aside and chalk it up to prevalent 1950’s thinking. I still enjoyed the broader story, the big concepts, and the excellent ending. I’m still an Asimov and Heinlein fan, it’s just a good reminder that they weren’t perfect. I think character development and development of believable relationships has improved in modern science fiction, but it doesn’t stop me from appreciating all that the classic sci-fi masters created.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9319423617173924054" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1245602708" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1245602708" />
    <a title="Jason" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17326212-jason"><img alt="Jason" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455316041p2/17326212.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1245602708?book_show_action=true">Apr 04, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason" href="/user/show/17326212-jason">Jason</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780765319197" href="/book/show/8263282-the-end-of-eternity">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1245602708" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1570579973472956595">In a way, it's unfortunate that so much of Asimov's fiction falls into his massive future history of Foundations and robots and galactic empires. And then, in the 80's, Asimov littered his canon with so many subpar prequels and refits and bridges that the few singletons he had were even further buried beneath the onslaught. And that's too bad, because The End of Eternity is the best novel Asimov ever wrote. <br/><br/>It has all the hallmarks of his best work, the supremely cool high concept, the strict lo</span>
  <span id="freeText1570579973472956595" style="display:none">In a way, it's unfortunate that so much of Asimov's fiction falls into his massive future history of Foundations and robots and galactic empires. And then, in the 80's, Asimov littered his canon with so many subpar prequels and refits and bridges that the few singletons he had were even further buried beneath the onslaught. And that's too bad, because The End of Eternity is the best novel Asimov ever wrote. <br /><br />It has all the hallmarks of his best work, the supremely cool high concept, the strict logic, the plot twists, the somehow compelling and addictive dialogue, all of that, except this time he uses more complex characterization than usual, more layered symbolism, and a stronger focus on interpersonal relationships between the characters. It is more suspenseful. The plot functions perfectly. The climax is shocking, and yet inevitable. Asimov used everything he had, and some of what he never used before or since, to create this masterful exploration of the cyclical nature of history, the ethics of plotting humanity's future, the questions surrounding what makes humanity thrive. <br /><br />If you're an Asimov fan, and you're checking out this page, my answer is yes, read this book. It'll give you that feeling you had when you first read Caves of Steel, or Foundation and Empire, but you won't have to read ten more books afterwards to find out what happens. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1570579973472956595" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1408097229" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1408097229" />
    <a title="Maria" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9511359-maria"><img alt="Maria" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591701402p2/9511359.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1408097229?book_show_action=true">Oct 04, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maria" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maria" href="/user/show/9511359-maria">Maria</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/13497588-the-end-of-eternity">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9511359-maria?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9511359-maria?shelf=2015">2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1408097229" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5561148039845076262">This book is amazing. I would say that Asimov is amazing too, but since I’ve only read <i>The End of Eternity</i>, and given the great amount of books that he wrote, that wouldn’t be fair. But nevertheless, the Asimov I saw there <i>is</i> amazing and that’s enough. <br/><br/>Short books are a dangerous thing. They seem the perfect choice if one wants to read but has little time, but may become an endless torment (like <i>Crash</i>) or a time-consuming obsession (like <i>The End of Eternity</i>). In both cases you <i>need</i> to end the bo</span>
  <span id="freeText5561148039845076262" style="display:none">This book is amazing. I would say that Asimov is amazing too, but since I’ve only read <i>The End of Eternity</i>, and given the great amount of books that he wrote, that wouldn’t be fair. But nevertheless, the Asimov I saw there <i>is</i> amazing and that’s enough. <br /><br />Short books are a dangerous thing. They seem the perfect choice if one wants to read but has little time, but may become an endless torment (like <i>Crash</i>) or a time-consuming obsession (like <i>The End of Eternity</i>). In both cases you <i>need</i> to end the book as soon as posible (for different reasons), but you also <i>don’t want</i> to do so (again, for different reasons). With <i>The End of Eternity</i>, I felt like I had to finish the book or otherwise I could not do anything else properly, but I could not imagine myself committing such a crime because it was such a great story and there were so many things I didn’t know and only twenty pages to have them explained. <br /><br />One thing that makes this book so <i>great</i> is, apart from the story itself, the ending. With a slightly different ending, <i>The End of Eternity</i> would have been an incredible piece of shit. Because the characters are good, and the story is very interesting and original, but it’s the end what makes it so beautifully <i>right</i>. It’s that sort of story that makes you feel terribly sad and happy at the same time, and a bit annoyed because the writer has played with you and with the characters and he doesn’t seem to care at all.<br /><br />I’m amazed at how Asimov could achieve <i>so much</i> with only 189 pages. I’ve read books four times this length with less than a quarter of its inventive and of the characters’ development. Definitely I will read more by Asimov, but I’m afraid to what I’ll find since I loved <i>The End of Eternity</i> so much… <br /><br />To summarise (and I won’t talk about the story because it’s too short to say something that is not an important spoiler), I would recommend it for many reasons and to many people, but I will leave it on just one question that has been on my mind since I began the book, and that now has an special meaning:<br /><br /><blockquote> What if we <i>once</i> had Eternity? </blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="5561148039845076262" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_471863406" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/471863406" />
    <a title="James Renner" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5686357-james-renner"><img alt="James Renner" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1493767277p2/5686357.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/471863406?book_show_action=true">Dec 06, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James Renner" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James Renner" href="/user/show/5686357-james-renner">James Renner</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer471863406" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12608748110326205833">I love going back to the classics. It’s fun to see what inspired modern-day novelists and screenwriters when they were kids. The End of Eternity, by Isaac Asimov, surely inspired cool stories like The Adjustment Bureau, Fringe, 12 Monkeys, Looper, and Primer.<br/><br/>Check it out: the story is told from the perspective of an “Observer” whose job it is to tweak history so that the aggregate majority of humans live in the highest achievable state of happiness. Just outside of Time is this place called Eter</span>
  <span id="freeText12608748110326205833" style="display:none">I love going back to the classics. It’s fun to see what inspired modern-day novelists and screenwriters when they were kids. The End of Eternity, by Isaac Asimov, surely inspired cool stories like The Adjustment Bureau, Fringe, 12 Monkeys, Looper, and Primer.<br /><br />Check it out: the story is told from the perspective of an “Observer” whose job it is to tweak history so that the aggregate majority of humans live in the highest achievable state of happiness. Just outside of Time is this place called Eternity, where numerous, emotionless monk-like middle management types toil away in an office complex, plotting reality changes to history. Observer Andrew Harlan actually becomes one of the trusted “Technicians” who must figure out the minimal changes necessary to put Time on the right course.<br /><br />Of course Harlan then encounters a young woman whose existence causes him to question the ethics of what he is doing.<br /><br />This has everything we’ve come to love about time travel stories: cool machines, mind-numbing paradoxes, and philosophical discussions about the power of “intention” — and it was written in 1955! Ultimately, though, it remains a very tight story about the education of Harlan. His gal breaks it down for him in the end: “In ironing out the disasters of Reality, Eternity rules out the triumphs as well. It is in meeting the great tests that mankind can most successfully rise to great heights.”<br /><br />There is also quite a nice twist ending I don’t want to spoil, involving a Galactic Empire. Trust me, it fits. It’s short and sweet so check it out.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12608748110326205833" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
